17/10/23, 7:55â€¯pm - Shrish: def bubblesort(list1):
    n = len(list1)
    for i in range(0, n - 1):
        for j in range(i, n - 1):
            if list1[j] > list1[j + 1]:
                list1[j], list1[j + 1] = list1[j + 1], list1[j]
    print(list1)
    new = list1
    print("\nTop five scores are:")
    for i in range(1, 6):
        print(new[-i])

def selectionsort(list1):
    n = len(list1)
    for i in range(0, n - 1):
        mini = list1[i]
        for j in range(i + 1, n):
            if mini > list1[j]:
                mini = list1[j]
                list1[i], list1[j] = list1[j], list1[i]
    return list1

n = int(input("Enter the number of students in class:\n"))
marks = []
print("Enter marks in percentage:")
for i in range(n):
    m = int(input())
    marks.append(m)
print(marks)

flag = 1
while flag == 1:
    print("Menu:\n1. Selection Sort\n2. Bubble Sort\n3. Exit")
    ch = int(input("Enter a choice:\n"))
    if ch == 1:
        print("Sorted list is:", selectionsort(marks))
    elif ch == 2:
        print("Sorted list is:")
        bubblesort(marks)
    elif ch == 3:
        flag = 0
        print("Thank you for using the program")

    if(start<last):
        i=start
        j=start+1
        while j<=last:
            if arr[j]<arr[start] :
                i+=1
                arr[i],arr[j]=arr[j],arr[i]
            j+=1
        arr[start],arr[i]=arr[i],arr[start]
        quicksort(arr,start,i-1)
        quicksort(arr,i+1,last)

30/10/23, 3:34â€¯pm - Shahid Sayyad: include<iostream>
#include<graphics.h>
using namespace std;
// Function to put pixels
// at subsequence points
void drawCircle(int xc, int yc, int x, int y)
{
    putpixel(xc+x, yc+y, RED);
    putpixel(xc-x, yc+y, RED);
    putpixel(xc+x, yc-y, RED);
    putpixel(xc-x, yc-y, RED);
    putpixel(xc+y, yc+x, RED);
    putpixel(xc-y, yc+x, RED);
    putpixel(xc+y, yc-x, RED);
    putpixel(xc-y, yc-x, RED);
}

// Function for circle-generation
// using Bresenham's algorithm
void circleBres(int xc, int yc, int r)
{
    int x = 0, y = r;
    int d = 3 - 2 * r;
    drawCircle(xc, yc, x, y);
    while (y >= x)
    {
        // for each pixel we will
        // draw all eight pixels

        x++;

        // check for decision parameter
        // and correspondingly
        // update d, x, y
        if (d > 0)
        {
            y--;
            d = d + 4 * (x - y) + 10;
        }
        else
            d = d + 4 * x + 6;
        drawCircle(xc, yc, x, y);
        delay(50);
    }
}


// Driver code
int main()
{
    int xc = 100, yc = 100, r = 60;
    int gd = DETECT, gm;
    initgraph(&gd, &gm, NULL);  // initialize graph
    circleBres(xc, yc, r);    // function call
    return 0;
}

01/11/23, 8:22â€¯pm - Shrish: #include <iostream>
#include <string>
using namespace std;

class Student
{
    string name;
    int roll_no;
    float cgpa;
    string contact;

public:
    Student()
    {
        name = "";
        roll_no = 0;
        cgpa = 0.0;
    }
    void getdata()
    {
        cout << "Enter Name: ";
        cin >> name;
        cout << "Enter Roll No.: ";
        cin >> roll_no;
        cout << "Enter CGPA: ";
        cin >> cgpa;
        cout << "Enter Contact No.: ";
        cin >> contact;
    }

    void display()
    {
        cout << "The name of the student is: " << name << endl;
        cout << "The roll no of the student is: " << roll_no << endl;
        cout << "The CGPA of the student is: " << cgpa << endl;
        cout << "The Contact No. of the student is: " << contact << endl;
    }

    int search(int r)
    {
        if (roll_no == r)
        {
            return 1;
        }
        else
        {
            return 0;
        }
    }

    void updatename(string s)
    {
        name = s;
    }
    void updatecgpa(float s)
    {
        cgpa = s;
    }
    void updatecontact(string s)
    {
        contact = s;
    }
};

int main()
{
    int flag, op, up, r, a, ro, n;
    string s, ch, co, y = "yes";
    float cg;

    cout << "Enter the number of students: ";
    cin >> n;
    Student students[10];

    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++)
    {
        cout << "For student s" << i + 1 << ":\n";
        students[i].getdata();
    }

    flag = 1;
    while (flag == 1)
    {
        cout << "Menu:\n1. Display\n2. Search\n3. Update\n4. Delete\n";
        cout << "Enter a choice: ";
        cin >> op;

        switch (op)
        {
        case 1:
            for (int i = 0; i < n; i++)
            {
                cout << "Student s" << i + 1 << ":\n";
                students[i].display();
            }
            break;

        case 2:
            cout << "Enter a roll no you want to search: ";
            cin >> r;
            a = 0;
            for (int i = 0; i < n; i++)
            {
                a = students[i].search(r);
                if (a == 1)
                {
                    cout << "Match found\n";
                    students[i].display();

                    break;
                }
            }
            if (a == 0)
            {
                cout << "Match not found\n";
            }
            break;

        case 3:
            int rc;
            cout << "Enter roll number of the student you want to change the data: ";
            cin >> rc;

            a = 0;
            for (int i = 0; i < n; i++)
            {
                a = students[i].search(rc);
                if (a == 1)
                {
                    cout << "Menu:\n1.Name\n2.CGPA\n3.Contact No.\n";
                    cout << "Enter a choice: ";
                    cin >> up;
                    switch (up)
                    {
                    case 1:
                        cout << "Enter a new name: ";
                        cin >> s;
                        students[i].updatename(s);
                        break;

                    case 2:
                        cout << "Enter a new CGPA: ";
                        cin >> cg;
                        students[i].updatecgpa(cg);
                        break;
                    case 3:
                        cout << "Enter a new Contact No.: ";
                        cin >> co;
                        students[i].updatecontact(co);
                        break;
                    }
                }
                else
                {
                    cout << "Invalid roll number. Please enter a valid roll number.\n";
                }
                break;
            }
        case 4:
            cout << "Enter the roll no of the student you want to delete: ";
            cin >> r;
            for (int i = 0; i < n; i++)
            {
                a = students[i].search(r);
                if (a == 1)
                {
                    n = n - 1;
                    for (int j = i; j < n; j++)
                    {
                        students[j] = students[j + 1];
                    }
                }
            }
            cout << "Student with roll no " << r << " deleted.\n";
            break;

        default:
            cout << "Invalid choice\n";
        }
        cout << "Do you want to continue (yes or no): ";
        cin >> ch;
        if (ch != y)
        {
            flag = 0;
            cout << "Thank you for using program.";
        }
    }
    return 0;
}

using namespace std;
class complex
{
    private:
     int a,b;
    public:
     
    complex()
    {
        a=0;
        b=0;
    
    }
    complex(complex &c1)
    {
        a=c1.a;
        b=c1.b;
    
    }
    complex(int c,int d)
    {
    a=c;
    b=d;
    }
    

    complex operator+(complex cc)
    {
        complex c;
        c.a= a+ cc.a;
        c.b=b+cc.b;
        return c;
    }
    complex operator-(complex cc)
    {
        complex c;
        c.a= a- cc.a;
        c.b=b-cc.b;
        return c;
    }
    complex operator+(int n)
    {
        complex c;
        c.a= a+ n;
        c.b=b+n;
        return c;
    }

    complex operator*(complex cc)
    {
        complex c;
        c.a=a*cc.a-b*cc.b;
        c.b=a*cc.b+b*cc.a;
        return c;   
    }
    complex operator/(complex cc)
    {
        complex c;
        c.a=a*cc.a+b*cc.b;
        c.b=a*cc.b-b*cc.a;
        return c;   
    }
     
    friend ostream& operator<<(ostream&, complex&);
    friend istream& operator>>(istream&,complex&);
    friend complex operator-(complex a,int n);
    
};
 ostream& operator<<(ostream& os,complex& c)
 {if(c.b<0)
 {os<<c.a<<""<<c.b;}
 else
  {os<<c.a<<"+"<<c.b;}
 return os;}
 istream& operator>>(istream& is,complex& c)
 {cout<<"\n real part";
 cin>>c.a;
cout<<"\nenter imaginary part";
 cin>>c.b;
 return cin;}


 complex operator-(complex x,int n)
{ complex c;
        c.a= x.a- n;
        c.b=x.b-n;
        return c;
    }

class node
{
    public:
    char bk_st;
    node* prev;
    node* next;
};
class theatre
{
    public:
    node* head;
     node* last;

    void create()
   { node* n;
   for(int i=0;i<7;i++)
   {
    n= new node();
    n->bk_st=' ';
    n->prev=0;
    if(head==NULL)
    {
        head=n;
        n->next=head;}
    else{
        node* temp1,*temp2;
        temp1=head;
        while(temp1->next!=head)
            {
            temp2=temp1;
            temp1=temp1->next;   
            temp1->next=n;
		n->prev=temp1;
		n->next=head;
  		temp1->prev=temp2;
            }
        }
    }
   }
   void show()
   {
    int i;
    node* temp=head;
    for(i=0;i<7;i++)
    if(temp!=NULL)
    {
        cout<<"("<<temp->bk_st<<")"<<"\t";

    temp=temp->next;
    }

        temp=temp->next;

30/11/23, 8:22â€¯am - Shrish: #include <iostream>
using namespace std;

class node
{
public:
    string name;
    int PRN;
    node *next;

    node()
    {
        name = "";
        PRN = 0;
        next = NULL;
    }
};

void create(node *&head, int n)
{
    cout << "Enter club member details:\n";
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++)
    {
        cout << "Enter PRN : ";
        node *temp = new node;
        cin >> temp->PRN;
        cout << "Enter name : ";
        cin >> temp->name;

        if (head == NULL)
        {
            head = temp;
        }
        else
        {
            node *last = head;
            while (last->next != NULL)
            {
                last = last->next;
            }
            last->next = temp;
        }
    }
}

void display(node *&head)
{
    node *temp = head;
    while (temp != NULL)
    {
        cout << temp->name << " (" << temp->PRN << ")\n";
        temp = temp->next;
    }
    cout << "\n";
}

void prepend(node *&head, int prn, string name)
{
    node *n = new node;
    n->PRN = prn;
    n->name = name;
    n->next = head;
    head = n;
}

void append(node *&head, int prn, string name)
{
    node *n = new node;
    n->PRN = prn;
    n->name = name;
    n->next = NULL;

    if (head == NULL)
    {
        head = n;
    }
    else
    {
        node *temp = head;
        while (temp->next != NULL)
        {
            temp = temp->next;
        }
        temp->next = n;
    }
}

void insert(node *&head, int key)
{
    node *n = new node;
    n->PRN = key;
    cout << "Enter name : ";
    cin >> n->name;

    if (head == NULL)
    {
        head = n;
        n->next = NULL;
    }
    else if (key == 0)
    {
        prepend(head, key, n->name);
    }
    else
    {
        node *temp = head;
        while (temp->next != NULL && temp->next->PRN != key)
        {
            temp = temp->next;
        }
        n->next = temp->next;
        temp->next = n;
    }
}

void delet(node *&head, int key)
{
    if (head == NULL)
    {
        cout << "List is empty. Deletion not possible.\n";
        return;
    }

    node *curr = head;
    node *n = head->next;

    if (key == 0)
    {
        head = head->next;
        delete curr;
    }
    else
    {
        while (n != NULL && n->PRN != key)
        {
            n = n->next;
            curr = curr->next;
        }

        if (n == NULL)
        {
            cout << "Member with PRN " << key << " not found.\n";
        }
        else
        {
            curr->next = n->next;
            delete n;
            cout << "Member with PRN " << key << " deleted.\n";
        }
    }
}

int count(node *&head)
{
    int count = 0;
    node *temp = head;
    while (temp != NULL)
    {
        count++;
        temp = temp->next;
    }
    return count;
}

node *concatenate(node *&head1, node *&head2)
{
    node *temp1 = head1;
    while (temp1->next != NULL)
    {
        temp1 = temp1->next;
    }
    temp1->next = head2;
    return head1;
}

int main()
{
    int n1, n2, n3, op, cnt, prn;
    string name;
    node *head1 = NULL;
    node *head2 = NULL;
    node *head3 = NULL;

    cout << "Enter number of students in the club for division SE: ";
    cin >> n1;
    create(head1, n1);

    cout << "Enter number of students in the club for division TE: ";
    cin >> n2;
    create(head2, n2);

    cout << "Enter number of students in the club for division BE: ";
    cin >> n3;
    create(head3, n3);

    do
    {
        cout << "Menu:\n";
        cout << "1. Add Club Member\n";
        cout << "2. Add President\n";
        cout << "3. Add Secretary\n";
        cout << "4. Delete Club Member\n";
        cout << "5. Delete President\n";
        cout << "6. Delete Secretary\n";
        cout << "7. Display total count of club members\n";
        cout << "8. Display Members\n";
        cout << "9. Concatenate club members of three divisions\n";
        cout << "10. Exit\n";

        cout << "Enter a choice: ";
        cin >> op;

        switch (op)
        {
        case 1:
            cout << "Division:\n1. SE\n2. TE\n3. BE\n";
            cout << "Enter a choice: ";
            cin >> op;

            switch (op)
            {
            case 1:
                cout << "Enter PRN: ";
                cin >> prn;
                insert(head1, prn);
                break;
            case 2:
                cout << "Enter PRN: ";
                cin >> prn;
                insert(head2, prn);
                break;
            case 3:
                cout << "Enter PRN: ";
                cin >> prn;
                insert(head3, prn);
                break;
            default:
                cout << "Invalid choice\n";
                break;
            }
            break;

        case 2:
            cout << "Enter PRN for President: ";
            cin >> prn;
            cout << "Enter name for President: ";
            cin >> name;
            prepend(head1, prn, name);
            break;

        case 3:
            cout << "Enter PRN for Secretary: ";
            cin >> prn;
            cout << "Enter name for Secretary: ";
            cin >> name;
            append(head1, prn, name);
            break;

        case 4:
            cout << "Enter PRN to delete: ";
            cin >> prn;
            delet(head1, prn);
            break;

        case 5:
            delet(head1, 0);
            break;

        case 6:
            delet(head1, 1);
            break;

        case 7:
            cnt = count(head1) + count(head2) + count(head3);
            cout << "Total number of club members: " << cnt << endl;
            break;

        case 8:
            cout << "Division:\n1. SE\n2. TE\n3. BE\n";
            cout << "Enter a choice: ";
            cin >> op;

            switch (op)
            {
            case 1:
                display(head1);
                break;
            case 2:
                display(head2);
                break;
            case 3:
                display(head3);
                break;
            default:
                cout << "Invalid choice\n";
                break;
            }
            break;

        case 9:
            head1 = concatenate(head1, head2);
            head1 = concatenate(head1, head3);
            cout << "Club members concatenated.\n";
            break;

        case 10:
            cout << "Exiting program.\n";
            break;

        default:
            cout << "Invalid choice\n";
            break;
        }

    } while (op != 10);

    return 0;
}

#include <graphics.h>
#include "snakeBody.h"
#include "food.h"

int main()
{
    initwindow(800,510,"SNAKE GAME");
    snakeBody body;
    food fruit;
    int length,count=0;
    bool playing=true;
    char lengtharr[3];
    fruit.generate(body.getPosx(), body.getPosy());
    int page=0;

    while(1)
    {
            setactivepage(page);
            setvisualpage(1-page);
            cleardevice();
            setcolor(BLUE);

            setfillstyle(SOLID_FILL, BLUE);


            if(GetAsyncKeyState(VK_LEFT))
            {    body.changeDirTo(LEFT);    }
            if(GetAsyncKeyState(VK_UP))
            {    body.changeDirTo(UP);      }
            if(GetAsyncKeyState(VK_RIGHT))
            {    body.changeDirTo(RIGHT);   }
            if(GetAsyncKeyState(VK_DOWN))
            {    body.changeDirTo(DOWN);    }
            if(GetAsyncKeyState(VK_ESCAPE))
                  break;

            if(playing==true && !body.update())
            {
                  playing = false;
            }
            body.drawSnake();


            if(fruit.update(body.getPosx(), body.getPosy()))
            {
                 fruit.generate(body.getPosx(), body.getPosy());
                 body.appendSnake();
            }

            // BOXES
                 setcolor(BLUE);
                 rectangle(0,0,30,510);
                 rectangle(30,0,480,30);
                 rectangle(480,0,510,510);
                 rectangle(30,480,480,510);
                 rectangle(510,0,800,20);
                 rectangle(510,250,800,270);
                 rectangle(780,20,800,250);
                 rectangle(510,490,800,510);
                 rectangle(780,270,800,490);
                 setfillstyle(SOLID_FILL, BLUE);
                 floodfill(15,250,BLUE);
                 floodfill(250,15,BLUE);
                 floodfill(495,250,BLUE);
                 floodfill(250,495,BLUE);
                 floodfill(550,260,BLUE);
                 floodfill(550,10,BLUE);
                 floodfill(790,150,BLUE);
                 floodfill(550,500,BLUE);
                 floodfill(790,300,BLUE);

            // SCORE
                 settextstyle(SANS_SERIF_FONT, HORIZ_DIR, 5);
                 setcolor(GREEN);
                 outtextxy(550, 70, "LENGTH");
                 outtextxy(520, 130, "ACHIEVED");
                 length = body.getlength();
                 lengtharr[0]=char(48+length/10);
                 lengtharr[1]=char(48+length%10);
                 lengtharr[2]='\0';
                 outtextxy(620, 190, lengtharr);


            // STATUS
                 settextstyle(SANS_SERIF_FONT, HORIZ_DIR, 4);
                 outtextxy(520, 300, "STATUS :-");
                 if (body.getlength() == 30)
                 {
                      outtextxy(520, 350, "YOU WON !");
                      playing=false;
                 }
                 else if(playing)
                 {
                      outtextxy(520, 350, "PLAYING");
                 }
                 else
                 {
                      outtextxy(520, 350, "GAME OVER");
                 }
                 settextstyle(SANS_SERIF_FONT, HORIZ_DIR, 2);
                 outtextxy(520,400,"PRESS 'ESC' to EXIT");


            fruit.draw();
            page = 1-page;
            delay(100);
    }

    getch();
    closegraph();
}

02/12/23, 9:29â€¯am - Shrish: #include <iostream>
#include <windows.h>
#include <graphics.h>

using namespace std;

const int width = 20;
const int height = 20;

int length = 0;
int score = 0;
int gameover = 0;

int snakeX[100], snakeY[100];
int fruitX, fruitY;

int direction; // 1 - up, 2 - down, 3 - left, 4 - right

void setup()
{
    gameover = 0;
    direction = 4; // initially moving right
    length = 1;
    snakeX[0] = width / 2;
    snakeY[0] = height / 2;
    fruitX = rand() % width;
    fruitY = rand() % height;
}

void draw()
{
    char buffer[10]; // Buffer for converting int to char array
    cleardevice(); // Clears the graphics screen

    // Draw Snake
    for (int i = 0; i < length; i++)
        rectangle(snakeX[i] * 20, snakeY[i] * 20, (snakeX[i] + 1) * 20, (snakeY[i] + 1) * 20);

    // Draw Fruit
    rectangle(fruitX * 20, fruitY * 20, (fruitX + 1) * 20, (fruitY + 1) * 20);

    // Display score
    sprintf(buffer, "Score: %d", score);
    outtextxy(10, height * 20 + 10, buffer);

    // Display game over message
    if (gameover)
        outtextxy(width * 10 - 50, height * 10 - 10, const_cast<char*>("Game Over!"));
}

void input()
{
    if (GetAsyncKeyState('W') & 0x8000)
        direction = 1;
    else if (GetAsyncKeyState('S') & 0x8000)
        direction = 2;
    else if (GetAsyncKeyState('A') & 0x8000)
        direction = 3;
    else if (GetAsyncKeyState('D') & 0x8000)
        direction = 4;
    else if (GetAsyncKeyState('X') & 0x8000)
        gameover = 1;
}

void algorithm()
{
    // Move the snake
    for (int i = length - 1; i > 0; i--)
    {
        snakeX[i] = snakeX[i - 1];
        snakeY[i] = snakeY[i - 1];
    }

    // Change head position based on direction
    switch (direction)
    {
    case 1:
        snakeY[0]--;
        break;
    case 2:
        snakeY[0]++;
        break;
    case 3:
        snakeX[0]--;
        break;
    case 4:
        snakeX[0]++;
        break;
    }

    // Check for collision with walls
    if (snakeX[0] >= width || snakeX[0] < 0 || snakeY[0] >= height || snakeY[0] < 0)
        gameover = 1;

    // Check for collision with itself
    for (int i = 1; i < length; i++)
    {
        if (snakeX[0] == snakeX[i] && snakeY[0] == snakeY[i])
            gameover = 1;
    }

    // Check for collision with fruit
    if (snakeX[0] == fruitX && snakeY[0] == fruitY)
    {
        score += 10;
        length++;
        fruitX = rand() % width;
        fruitY = rand() % height;
    }
}

int main()
{
    int gd = DETECT, gm;
    initgraph(&gd, &gm, NULL);

    setup();

    while (!gameover)
    {
        draw();
        input();
        algorithm();
        Sleep(100); // Adjust the speed of the snake
    }

    delay(500); // Pause for a moment before exiting

    closegraph();
    return 0;
}

02/12/23, 12:25â€¯pm - Shahid Sayyad: A snake game is a classic video game that involves a growing line represented as a snake that can move around the screen, eat items, and avoid obstacles. The game is usually played on a rectangular grid, where the snake can wrap around the edges. The game ends when the snake collides with itself or an obstacle. The goal of the game is to make the snake as long as possible by eating items that appear randomly on the grid.

There are different ways to implement a snake game using C++ graphics. One of the most common libraries used for graphics programming in C++ is Graphics.h, which provides functions for drawing shapes, colors, text, and images on the screen. Graphics.h is a header file that is included in the Turbo C++ compiler, which is an old and outdated compiler that is not compatible with modern operating systems. However, it is still possible to use Graphics.h on newer platforms by using an emulator or a wrapper library that mimics its functionality.

To create a snake game using Graphics.h, one needs to follow these steps:

- Initialize the graphics mode and the screen size using the initgraph() function.
- Define the constants and variables for the game logic, such as the width and height of the grid, the size and color of the snake and the items, the direction and speed of the snake, the score and the game state, etc.
- Create a function to draw the grid on the screen using the line() and setcolor() functions.
- Create a function to draw the snake on the screen using the rectangle() and setfillstyle() functions. The snake can be represented as an array of coordinates that store the position of each segment of the snake. The first element of the array is the head of the snake, and the last element is the tail. To move the snake, one needs to update the coordinates of each segment according to the direction of the snake, and add a new segment at the front of the array when the snake eats an item.
- Create a function to draw the items on the screen using the circle() and setfillstyle() functions. The items can be represented as random coordinates that are generated within the grid. To check if the snake has eaten an item, one needs to compare the coordinates of the head of the snake with the coordinates of the item, and if they match, increase the score and generate a new item.
- Create a function to check the collision of the snake with itself or the edges of the grid using the getpixel() function. The getpixel() function returns the color of the pixel at a given coordinate. To check if the snake has collided with itself, one needs to compare the color of the pixel at the head of the snake with the color of the snake. To check if the snake has collided with the edges of the grid, one needs to compare the coordinates of the head of the snake with the boundaries of the grid. If either of these conditions is true, set the game state to over and display a game over message on the screen.
- Create a function to handle the keyboard input from the user using the kbhit() and getch() functions. The kbhit() function returns true if a key is pressed, and the getch() function returns the ASCII code of the pressed key. To change the direction of the snake, one needs to map the arrow keys to the corresponding direction values, and update the direction variable accordingly. To pause or resume the game, one needs to map the spacebar key to the corresponding game state values, and update the game state variable accordingly.
- Create a main function that contains the game loop, which consists of the following steps:
    - Call the function to draw the grid on the screen.
    - Call the function to draw the snake on the screen.
    - Call the function to draw the items on the screen.
    - Call the function to check the collision of the snake.
    - Call the function to handle the keyboard input from the user.
    - Delay the execution of the loop for a certain amount of time using the delay() function, which depends on the speed of the snake.
    - Clear the screen using the cleardevice() function.
    - Repeat the loop until the game state is over.

This is a basic outline of how to create a snake game using C++ graphics. There are many ways to improve and enhance the game, such as adding sound effects, music, animations, levels, difficulty, etc. For more details and examples of how to implement a snake game using C++ graphics, you can refer to the following web sources:

- [Snake Code in C++ - GeeksforGeeks](^1^)
- [Snake Code in C++ - javatpoint](^2^)

08/03/24, 10:04â€¯pm - Shrish: Low Complexity: 3 Function Points (FP)
Average Complexity: 4 Function Points (FP)
High Complexity: 6 Function Points (FP)
Now, let's assign complexity levels to each function:

External Inputs (EI):

User login and authentication (Average Complexity): 4 FP
QR code scanning for passenger login (Average Complexity): 4 FP
Distraction detection inputs (High Complexity): 6 FP
Traffic sign inputs (High Complexity): 6 FP
External Outputs (EO):

Audio alerts for distraction detection (Average Complexity): 4 FP
Audio alerts for traffic sign recognition (Average Complexity): 4 FP
External Inquiries (EQ):

Database queries for storing and retrieving scenic images (Average Complexity): 4 FP
Retrieving past alerts and driving data (Average Complexity): 4 FP
Internal Logical Files (ILF):

Database for storing driver and passenger information (Average Complexity): 4 FP
Database for storing captured images (Average Complexity): 4 FP
External Interface Files (EIF):

Interface with camera systems for input data (Average Complexity): 4 FP
Interface with audio system for output alerts (Average Complexity): 4 FP
Now, let's calculate the Total Function Points (TFP):

TFP = EI + EO + EQ + ILF + EIF

TFP = (4 + 4 + 6 + 6) + (4 + 4) + (4 + 4) + (4 + 4)

TFP = 42 Function Points

11/03/24, 6:07â€¯pm - Shahid Sayyad: Certainly! Here's a simplified feasibility analysis for an app involving driver traffic signs and surrounding detection:

1. **Market Demand**: There is a growing demand for apps that enhance driver safety and awareness on the road. Drivers are increasingly looking for tools to help them navigate safely and efficiently.

2. **Technology**: Advances in computer vision and machine learning make it feasible to develop algorithms capable of detecting traffic signs and surrounding objects in real-time using smartphone cameras.

3. **Data Availability**: Labeled datasets for training machine learning models on traffic sign detection are readily available, although collecting data for surrounding object detection may require additional effort.

4. **Regulatory Compliance**: The app must comply with local regulations regarding driver assistance systems. It should not distract drivers or violate any laws related to smartphone use while driving.

5. **Competitive Analysis**: While there may be existing apps with similar functionalities, the unique selling points of this app, such as its accuracy in detecting traffic signs and surroundings, could make it stand out in the market.

6. **Monetization Strategy**: The app could be monetized through a combination of app purchases, subscriptions for premium features, and partnerships with insurance companies or car manufacturers.

7. **Development Costs**: Development costs would include software development, data acquisition, testing, and ongoing maintenance. However, these costs can be managed by leveraging open-source technologies and cloud computing services.

8. **User Experience**: A user-friendly interface and intuitive design are crucial for the app's success. Conducting user testing and incorporating user feedback during development will be essential to ensure a positive user experience.

9. **Scalability**: The app should be designed to scale effectively as the user base grows. This includes considerations for server infrastructure, data processing capabilities, and customer support.

10. **Risk Analysis**: Potential risks include technical challenges in real-time detection, competition from established apps, and regulatory changes affecting driver assistance systems. Mitigation strategies should be in place to address these risks.

Overall, the feasibility analysis suggests that developing an app involving driver traffic signs and surrounding detection is feasible, given the demand, available technology, and strategic planning to address potential challenges.

11/03/24, 6:10â€¯pm - Shahid Sayyad: <Media omitted>
11/03/24, 6:11â€¯pm - Shahid Sayyad: YOLO (You Only Look Once) is a popular object detection algorithm known for its speed and accuracy. Some advantages of using YOLO modeling include:

1. **Real-time Detection**: YOLO can detect objects in real-time, making it suitable for applications where speed is crucial, such as autonomous vehicles, surveillance systems, and augmented reality.

2. **Single Forward Pass**: YOLO performs object detection in a single forward pass of the neural network, allowing it to process images quickly without the need for complex post-processing steps.

3. **High Accuracy**: Despite its speed, YOLO achieves high accuracy in object detection tasks, thanks to its carefully designed architecture and loss function.

4. **Object Localization**: YOLO not only detects objects but also provides precise bounding box coordinates for each detected object, making it useful for tasks requiring accurate object localization.

5. **Unified Framework**: YOLO is a unified framework that predicts multiple bounding boxes and class probabilities simultaneously, making it efficient and easy to implement.

6. **Versatility**: YOLO can be applied to a wide range of object detection tasks, including detecting traffic signs, pedestrians, vehicles, animals, and more, making it a versatile choice for various applications.

7. **Low False Positive Rate**: YOLO tends to have a lower false positive rate compared to other object detection algorithms, reducing the likelihood of incorrectly detecting objects that are not present in the image.

8. **Minimal Training Data**: YOLO requires relatively less training data compared to some other object detection methods, making it easier to train models for specific applications or domains with limited data availability.

9. **Open-source Implementations**: There are several open-source implementations of YOLO available, making it accessible to developers and researchers for experimentation and deployment in various projects.

Overall, YOLO offers a compelling combination of speed, accuracy, and versatility, making it a popular choice for object detection tasks in computer vision applications.

26/03/24, 9:09â€¯pm - Shrish: Prog. Paradigms
27/03/24, 12:40â€¯pm - Shrish: In a simplified manner, let's discuss the impact of machine architecture on programming languages:

Hardware:
Instruction Set: Programming languages need to align with the instructions supported by the hardware. For example, if the hardware supports floating-point arithmetic, the programming language should have constructs to utilize it efficiently.

Memory Management: Hardware architecture determines how memory is organized and accessed. Programming languages need to provide mechanisms to manage memory effectively, considering factors like cache hierarchy and memory addressing modes.

Concurrency and Parallelism: Modern hardware often includes multiple cores or processing units. Programming languages must provide features to exploit parallelism and concurrency, such as threading and synchronization primitives.

Firmware:
Low-Level Control: Firmware interacts closely with hardware components. Programming languages for firmware development need to offer low-level control over hardware, with features like direct memory access and hardware-specific optimizations.

Efficiency and Size: Firmware programs often run on resource-constrained devices with limited memory and processing power. Programming languages should be efficient and produce compact code to fit within these constraints.

Software:
Abstraction: Programming languages abstract away hardware complexities, allowing developers to focus on solving problems rather than worrying about hardware details. High-level languages provide this abstraction, making software development more accessible.

Portability: Software written in a programming language should ideally be portable across different hardware architectures. This requires the language to provide platform-independent abstractions and standard libraries.

Performance: Programming languages should enable developers to write efficient code that makes optimal use of hardware resources. This may involve features like compiler optimizations, inline assembly, and efficient data structures.

Integration: Software often needs to interact with hardware components and firmware. Programming languages should provide mechanisms for seamless integration, such as system calls, APIs, and hardware abstraction layers.

06/05/24, 5:42â€¯pm - Shahid Sayyad: #include <iostream>
#include <fstream>
#include <string>

using namespace std;

// Structure to represent student information
struct Student {
    int rollNumber;
    string name;
    string division;
    string address;
};

// Function prototypes
void addStudent();
void deleteStudent();
void displayStudent();

int main() {
    int choice;

    do {
        cout << "\nStudent Information System" << endl;
        cout << "1. Add Student\n2. Delete Student\n3. Display Student\n4. Exit\n";
        cout << "Enter your choice: ";
        cin >> choice;

        switch (choice) {
            case 1:
                addStudent();
                break;
            case 2:
                deleteStudent();
                break;
            case 3:
                displayStudent();
                break;
            case 4:
                cout << "Exiting program.\n";
                break;
            default:
                cout << "Invalid choice. Please try again.\n";
                break;
        }
    } while (choice != 4);

    return 0;
}

// Function to add a student to the file
void addStudent() {
    ofstream file("student_info.txt", ios::app);
    Student student;

    cout << "Enter Roll Number: ";
    cin >> student.rollNumber;
    cout << "Enter Name: ";
    cin.ignore();
    getline(cin, student.name);
    cout << "Enter Division: ";
    getline(cin, student.division);
    cout << "Enter Address: ";
    getline(cin, student.address);

    file << student.rollNumber << "," << student.name << "," << student.division << "," << student.address << endl;

    file.close();
    cout << "Student added successfully.\n";
}

// Function to delete a student from the file
void deleteStudent() {
    ifstream inFile("student_info.txt");
    ofstream outFile("temp.txt");
    int rollNumber;
    bool found = false;
    Student student;

    cout << "Enter Roll Number of student to delete: ";
    cin >> rollNumber;

    while (inFile >> student.rollNumber >> student.name >> student.division >> student.address) {
        if (student.rollNumber != rollNumber) {
            outFile << student.rollNumber << "," << student.name << "," << student.division << "," << student.address << endl;
        } else {
            found = true;
        }
    }

    inFile.close();
    outFile.close();

    remove("student_info.txt");
    rename("temp.txt", "student_info.txt");

    if (found) {
        cout << "Student with Roll Number " << rollNumber << " deleted successfully.\n";
    } else {
        cout << "Student not found.\n";
    }
}

// Function to display student information
void displayStudent() {
    ifstream file("student_info.txt");
    int rollNumber;
    bool found = false;
    Student student;

    cout << "Enter Roll Number of student to display: ";
    cin >> rollNumber;

    while (file >> student.rollNumber >> student.name >> student.division >> student.address) {
        if (student.rollNumber == rollNumber) {
            found = true;
            cout << "Roll Number: " << student.rollNumber << endl;
            cout << "Name: " << student.name << endl;
            cout << "Division: " << student.division << endl;
            cout << "Address: " << student.address << endl;
            break;
        }
    }

    file.close();

    if (!found) {
        cout << "Student not found.\n";
    }
}

06/10/24, 12:57â€¯am - Shahid Sayyad: Given that your project is a music app storing around 50-100 songs per user, here are some considerations for deploying on a free tier like PythonAnywhere:

1. Storage Capacity:

PythonAnywhereâ€™s free tier provides limited disk space (usually around 512 MB), which might not be sufficient for storing a large number of audio files.

For media storage, youâ€™ll likely need to use an external storage service, such as:

Amazon S3 (Free tier)

Google Cloud Storage (Free tier)

DigitalOcean Spaces (Free tier options available)



2. Performance & User Capacity:

With 50-100 songs per user, the database size and query complexity could grow, especially with multiple users.

PythonAnywhereâ€™s free tier may handle small-scale traffic and light database operations well, but heavy file uploads/downloads and concurrent requests could slow down the performance.


3. Scaling:

For a music app, where media file handling is critical, free hosting platforms like PythonAnywhere might not be the best long-term solution due to their limited resources and performance restrictions.

Consider using cloud storage for media files and offloading heavy tasks to a service with better scaling options.


Recommendation:

Host your Django app on PythonAnywhere (free tier) but store your music files on an external service like Amazon S3 for better performance and scalability.

11/10/24, 8:10â€¯am - Shahid Sayyad: import tkinter as tk
from tkinter import messagebox
from PIL import Image, ImageTk

def create_gradient(canvas, width, height, color1, color2):
    """Create a vertical gradient from color1 to color2."""
    for i in range(height):
        r = int(color1[0] + (color2[0] - color1[0]) * (i / height))
        g = int(color1[1] + (color2[1] - color1[1]) * (i / height))
        b = int(color1[2] + (color2[2] - color1[2]) * (i / height))
        color = f'#{r:02x}{g:02x}{b:02x}'
        canvas.create_line(0, i, width, i, fill=color)

def on_search():
    """Function to handle search button click."""
    query = search_entry.get()
    if query.strip():
        messagebox.showinfo("Search", f"You searched for: {query}")
    else:
        messagebox.showwarning("Input Error", "Please enter a search query.")

# Create the main window
root = tk.Tk()
root.title("Logo and Search Box with Gradient Background")

# Set the dimensions of the root window
root.geometry("1000x600")  # Width x Height

# Create a Canvas for the gradient background
canvas = tk.Canvas(root, width=1000, height=600)  # Match canvas size to the root window
canvas.pack(fill=tk.BOTH, expand=True)

# Create the gradient (from Pinkish Orange to Medium Purple)
create_gradient(canvas, 1000, 600, (255, 127, 80), (167, 123, 202))  # RGB values for colors

# Create a frame to hold the logo and search box
frame = tk.Frame(root, width=1000, height=50, bg='white')  # Set frame background color
frame.pack_propagate(False)  
frame.place(relx=0.5, rely=0.0, anchor='n') 

# Load and resize the logo image
image_path = "C:\\Users\\hp\\Desktop\\songs\\logo_new.png"  # Update the path to your logo image
original_image = Image.open(image_path)  # Open the original image
resized_image = original_image.resize((100, 50), Image.LANCZOS)  # Resize to 100x50 pixels
logo_image = ImageTk.PhotoImage(resized_image)  # Convert the resized image for Tkinter

logo_label = tk.Label(frame, image=logo_image, bg='white')  # Set label background color
logo_label.pack(side=tk.LEFT, padx=5)  # Add padding to the left of the logo

# Create a search box (Entry widget)
search_entry = tk.Entry(frame, width=25, bg='white')  # Set entry background color
search_entry.pack(side=tk.RIGHT, padx=25)  # Pack the search entry after the button

search_button = tk.Button(frame, text="Search", bg='lightgray', command=on_search)
search_button.pack(side=tk.RIGHT, padx=15)  # Pack the search button first (rightmost)

# Create frame2
frame2 = tk.Frame(root, width=800, height=100, bg='lightgray')  # Adjust height for frame2
frame2.pack_propagate(False)  # Prevent frame from resizing to fit contents
frame2.place(relx=0.5, rely=0.1, anchor='n')  # Place frame2 below the logo frame

# Create a frame for the label and dropdown to align them horizontally
dropdown_frame = tk.Frame(frame2)
dropdown_frame.pack(anchor='w', padx=5, pady=5, fill=tk.X)  # Allow filling horizontally

# Label
label = tk.Label(dropdown_frame, text="All Songs", bg='lightgray', font=('Arial', 12))
label.pack(side=tk.LEFT)  # Pack label to the left

# Dropdown menu
dropdown_var = tk.StringVar(value="Actions")
option_menu = tk.OptionMenu(dropdown_frame, dropdown_var, "Add to playlist", "Share", "Delete")
option_menu.pack(side=tk.RIGHT)  # Pack dropdown to the right

# Fill space between label and dropdown
tk.Label(dropdown_frame, text="", width=40).pack(side=tk.LEFT, expand=True)  # Spacer to push dropdown to the right

# Create a frame for the listboxes
frame_listbox = tk.Frame(root, bg='white')  # Set a visible background color
frame_listbox.pack(fill=tk.BOTH, expand=True)

# Create header labels
tk.Label(frame_listbox, text="Song", bg='lightgray', font=('Arial', 12)).grid(row=0, column=0)
tk.Label(frame_listbox, text="Artist", bg='lightgray', font=('Arial', 12)).grid(row=0, column=1)
tk.Label(frame_listbox, text="Duration", bg='lightgray', font=('Arial', 12)).grid(row=0, column=2)

# Create Listboxes with a scrollbar
song_listbox = tk.Listbox(frame_listbox, height=15, width=30, bg='white', font=('Arial', 10))
artist_listbox = tk.Listbox(frame_listbox, height=15, width=30, bg='white', font=('Arial', 10))
duration_listbox = tk.Listbox(frame_listbox, height=15, width=20, bg='white', font=('Arial', 10))

song_listbox.grid(row=1, column=0, padx=5, pady=5)
artist_listbox.grid(row=1, column=1, padx=5, pady=5)
duration_listbox.grid(row=1, column=2, padx=5, pady=5)

# Populate Listboxes with the provided song data
songs = [
    ("7 Years", "Lukas Graham", "3:57"),
    ("Journey Song", "Shreya Ghoshal", "4:13"),
    ("Khalasi", "Aditya Gadhavi", "4:18"),
    ("Mere Ghar", "Ram Jubin Nautiyal", "3:54"),
    ("Tera Fitoor", "Arijit Singh", "5:10"),
    ("Oo Antav", "Indravathi Chauhan", "3:43"),
    ("Tu Jaane Na", "Pritam", "5:42")
]

for song, artist, duration in songs:
    song_listbox.insert(tk.END, song)
    artist_listbox.insert(tk.END, artist)
    duration_listbox.insert(tk.END, duration)

# Start the main event loop
root.mainloop()

19/10/24, 10:06â€¯pm - Shahid Sayyad: Yes, there are additional methods you can incorporate to enhance the system and reduce misrecognition and proxies in real time:

1. Gait Recognition

Solution: Use gait recognition in addition to facial recognition. Gait analysis examines how individuals walk and can serve as an extra layer of identity verification.

Real-time Impact: This makes it harder for a student to proxy for another, as gait patterns are difficult to mimic. If the gait and face data don't match, it will trigger an alert for manual verification.


2. AI-Based Attention Monitoring

Solution: Integrate a system to analyze whether the student is paying attention during the lecture (e.g., tracking head movements, eye contact, etc.). If the system notices erratic behavior (e.g., a student stops being recognized after a while), it can send a flag.

Real-time Impact: This helps prevent cases where proxies enter the class just to mark attendance and then leave or do not engage in class activities.


3. Multi-Factor Recognition

Solution: Use multiple biometric factors (e.g., facial recognition + voice recognition) to verify attendance. During the recognition process, students can also be prompted to respond to a quick voice prompt.

Real-time Impact: Proxies would have difficulty faking both a face and voice at the same time, reducing the chances of successful attendance fraud.


4. Time-of-Entry and Exit Correlation

Solution: Set a time correlation system where attendance is cross-referenced with entry and exit logs. If a student is marked as attending but leaves too soon, the system flags this as suspicious behavior.

Real-time Impact: The system prevents proxies who attempt to sign in for a class but leave right after attendance is marked.


5. Randomized Check-ins During Class

Solution: Implement random check-ins during the class session. The system can trigger a second or third facial recognition scan randomly during the lecture to ensure the student is still present.

Real-time Impact: Students would not know when the next check-in will happen, discouraging them from leaving after being marked as present or getting someone else to proxy.


6. Facial Recognition with Liveness Detection

Solution: Implement liveness detection along with facial recognition. Liveness detection uses techniques like blinking, head movement, or 3D depth perception to confirm that the face being scanned is not a photograph or video.

Real-time Impact: It prevents students from using photos, videos, or static images to bypass the system and proxy for someone else.


7. AI-Powered Behavioral Analysis

Solution: Use an AI-powered system to analyze behavioral patterns such as how a student interacts with devices, how frequently they check-in, or even patterns of attention throughout the session.

Real-time Impact: It adds an extra layer of verification, analyzing each studentâ€™s behavior and raising red flags for unusual activities that suggest proxying.


8. Face Mask Detection

Solution: Incorporate mask detection in case students are required to wear face masks in certain situations (e.g., due to health regulations). This ensures the system doesn't misrecognize a student due to obstruction of facial features.

Real-time Impact: Prevents attendance issues due to face coverings, ensuring accurate facial recognition even when masks are worn.


9. Camera Placement with Dynamic Tracking

Solution: Use multiple dynamic cameras with tracking capabilities that adjust their focus based on student movement. This helps to continuously monitor students entering and exiting, ensuring proper coverage and angles for all faces.

Real-time Impact: Minimizes blind spots and improves facial recognition accuracy by continuously keeping students in view, even if they move around the room.


10. Manual Confirmation for High-Risk Students

Solution: For students flagged by the system (due to multiple misrecognitions, prior proxy incidents, etc.), require a manual confirmation through an admin dashboard where an authorized person can manually verify attendance.

Real-time Impact: The system reduces errors by ensuring problematic cases are handled manually without affecting the automated attendance for the majority of students.


11. GPS-Based Geofencing

Solution: If students carry their mobile phones, you could incorporate geofencing technology to confirm their presence within the classroom area. A facial recognition match is only valid if the studentâ€™s phone is detected within a specific geographic boundary (e.g., the classroom).

Real-time Impact: This would prevent proxying attempts as the system will only mark attendance if the studentâ€™s physical location (tracked by GPS) matches the classroom.


12. Bluetooth Beacons

Solution: Deploy Bluetooth beacons in the classroom that detect students' smartphones. The system can cross-verify Bluetooth signals with facial recognition data to confirm the studentâ€™s presence.

Real-time Impact: This reduces the chances of proxying because the system will only recognize a student if both the facial recognition and Bluetooth signals from their phone match.


By combining some of these methods, you can greatly enhance the reliability and accuracy of your system while addressing issues related to misrecognition and proxies.
19/10/24, 10:06â€¯pm - Shahid Sayyad: To manage real-time misrecognition and proxies in your attendance system, youâ€™ll need to implement strategies that ensure high accuracy and enable immediate correction when errors occur. Here are some remedies:

1. Confidence Threshold for Recognition

Solution: Use a confidence score threshold in your facial recognition model. If the modelâ€™s confidence is below a certain threshold (e.g., 90%), trigger an alert or a manual verification process (like an admin check or secondary method).

Real-time Impact: The system won't automatically log attendance if itâ€™s uncertain about a match, reducing the chances of misrecognition.


2. Dual Verification Mechanism

Solution: Implement a secondary verification stepâ€”such as a fingerprint scanner, smart ID card, or mobile phone OTPâ€”to cross-check the identity. When a student is misrecognized, this secondary check can resolve the conflict.

Real-time Impact: If the system flags any uncertainty or students report misrecognition, they can authenticate themselves with a secondary method.


3. Face Re-authentication During Class

Solution: Periodically re-check the students in the class. The system can periodically re-scan students to verify that the people in the classroom at intervals are still the same.

Real-time Impact: If the system detects a mismatch in faces, it can flag the issue for manual review or notify the faculty.


4. Proxy Detection via Behavior Analysis

Solution: Implement a behavior-based detection system that looks for inconsistencies. For example:

Use a combination of location and time data to track if someone exits the classroom too early after attendance is marked.

Detect patterns of proxy behavior, such as students being in two locations at once.


Real-time Impact: You can integrate a rule-based system that flags suspicious behavior or abnormal patterns and alerts faculty or administrators.


5. Blacklist and Notification System

Solution: For frequent issues of misrecognition or proxies, you can maintain a blacklist of students who require manual attendance verification. Set up real-time notifications for faculty if a flagged student enters.

Real-time Impact: The system can immediately prompt a manual intervention or send an alert if a proxy attempt is detected, ensuring the situation is handled quickly.


6. Leverage Multiple Cameras for Enhanced Accuracy

Solution: Place multiple cameras (e.g., front and side) at different angles in the classroom to capture students from various perspectives. Misrecognition often happens due to limited camera angles, so additional views improve accuracy.

Real-time Impact: The system can cross-check multiple angles in real time and prevent students from proxying for others or misrecognition caused by poor camera placement.


7. Machine Learning Retraining and Continuous Learning

Solution: Implement continuous learning where the model is retrained periodically based on updated data (new facial features, updated student records). Regular retraining can minimize misrecognition.

Real-time Impact: The model improves over time and adapts to new students or changes in studentsâ€™ appearances (haircuts, glasses, etc.).


8. Manual Override System

Solution: If the system misrecognizes a student in real-time, allow faculty to manually override the recognition result using a simple interface. This can ensure that even if the system makes an error, itâ€™s corrected on the spot.

Real-time Impact: Misrecognition issues can be corrected immediately without affecting the attendance record.


9. Use of Wearable Devices

Solution: Issue wearable devices (like RFID bands or smart ID cards) that verify the student's identity when they enter the class. The system can cross-check facial recognition data with the RFID or smart card.

Real-time Impact: If a student tries to proxy for someone, the system will flag the mismatch between the face and the RFID data.



---

By implementing these remedies, you can mitigate both misrecognition and proxy issues in real time, ensuring that the attendance system remains accurate and secure.
